        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ecd3c0e32c0f4d88ba1b249d8b42f717">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '95f1e54f-2384-4fc0-8eb3-07402b651b7d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d57148003" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) materials with rich closed pore structures and nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer have emerged as promising anode in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, it still remains a tremendous challenge to precisely regulate closed pore structures and soft carbon coating thicknesses for achieving excellent electrochemical performance in SIBs at low-voltage platforms. Herein, PCHC-10 with abundant and suitable-sized closed pore size (0.45&nbsp;nm) and nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer has been accurately designed by chemical crosslink reaction between the pre-oxidized phenolic resin and a small addition of pitch to form ester-based bond. As anode, PCHC-10 delivered large reversible capacity of 359.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> within 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V, and high capacity of 242.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in low voltage platforms (≤0.15&nbsp;V). Besides, PCHC-10 anode exhibits 91.4% capacity retention for 100 cycles, and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//PCHC-10 full cell has superior rate performance and high energy density of 231.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the detailed electrochemical storage behaviors and theoretical calculations revealed that the HC owning closed pore-size of 0.45&nbsp;nm has the strongest Na<sup>+</sup> storage abilities in low-voltage platforms. This work presents a novel insight for constructing HC with suitable-sized closed pore structures and soft coating layer to boost Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability in low-voltage platforms.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403642-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403642-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as an effective complement to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to their advantages of abundant sodium resources, low cost, and similar operating principle to LIBs. However, there are still tremendous challenges in developing superior electrochemical properties anode materials with high capacity at low-voltage platforms (≤0.15&nbsp;V) and high initial coulombic efficiency (ICE) for commercial SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0001_R_d57147883e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater. J. Energy Chem. Engineering">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is widely recognized that the specific capacity in low-voltage and the ICE of the anode play crucial role in improving the energy power density of SIBs. In the past decades, numerous studies including carbon-based materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0002_R_d57147883e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Small">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloys,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0003_R_d57147883e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces J. Power Sources">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> organics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0004_R_d57147883e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Small">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and metal oxides<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0005_R_d57147883e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Electrochim. Acta">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been conducted to enhance the energy power density of SIBs. Among these, carbon-based materials are commonly considered to be the most ideal anode materials for SIBs due to their wide source and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0006_R_d57147883e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Sustain. Res.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially, hard carbon (HC) materials are expected to take the lead in the industrialized SIBs anode materials owing to the unique micro-morphological structures and stable electrochemical properties. Currently, it has been demonstrated that the capacity at low-voltage primarily derives from Na<sup>+</sup> storage within closed pore structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0007_R_d57147883e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, numerous studies have been mainly focused on enhancing the number of closed pores in HC materials. For example, Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0008_R_d57147883e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Carbon">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> introduced C(O)─O functional groups into oxidized asphalt at 1100&nbsp;°C to generate closed pore structures, resulting in an electrode with a plateau capacity of 171.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Zhang et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0009_R_d57147883e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> prepared HC materials with rich closed pore structures by using zinc gluconate as a template agent and C source, leading to an HC anode delivering a plateau capacity of 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Fan et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0010_R_d57147883e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> fabricated HC with abundant closed pore structures by using CO<sub>2</sub> as an etching agent to calcinate as-esterified starch, resulting in an HC anode exhibiting a high capacity of 351&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Although the aforementioned studies have successfully generated numerous closed pore structures, there remains a notable gap in understanding the contribution of closed pores size in HC to the capacity at low-voltage regions. Additionally, there are various methods to regulate the closed pore structures, such as chemical activation, introducing template agents, and calcinating soft/hard carbon mixture. Generally, the chemical-activation agents and template agents used include ZnO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0009_R_d57147883e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0011_R_d57147883e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MgO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0012_R_d57147883e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0013_R_d57147883e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Alloys Compd.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> KOH,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0014_R_d57147883e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Diamond Relat. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnAc<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0015_R_d57147883e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> etc., which can effectively increase the number and size of closed pores in HC. Unfortunately, these chemicals have inevitably caused some problems including high-cost, equipment corrosion, and environmental pollution, although the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity at low-voltage regions has been greatly enhanced in SIBs. As for the calcination of soft/hard carbon precursors, it has been regarded as a promising method for the preparation of commercial HC anode in SIBs due to its simple operation process, low cost, large production, and environmentally friendly. Moreover, the soft carbon coating layer can significantly reduce the defect structures on the surface of HC materials, thereby improving its conductivity and ICE. For example, Xu et al<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0016_R_d57147883e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> calcinated the lignite and sucrose mixture, which was used as soft carbon and HC precursors, respectively. During the calcination process, the chemical cross-linking reaction occurred between the sucrose and lignite, and formed carbon-based hybrid microcrystals, which can prevent the formation of ordered graphitic phases. As anode, the as-prepared HC material delivered a high Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity of 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at low-voltage plateaus. Although these reported HC materials with rich closed pore structures have delivered so-called excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance, they still deliver a limited plateau capacity at low-voltage regions. Furthermore, there is a scarcity of research dedicated to exploring the structure-function relationship between closed pore size and Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability at low-voltage platforms.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, PCHC-10 with a suitable closed pore size of 0.45&nbsp;nm, rich closed pore structures coupled with nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer, was accurately prepared through a chemical crosslink reaction between the pre-oxidized phenolic resin and small addition of pitch (10 wt%) during carbonization. The detailed multi-scale microstructures of the PCHC-10 sample have been comprehensively demonstrated through a variety of characterization techniques. As anode, PCHC-10 demonstrated outstanding performance in SIBs, exhibiting a high ICE of 74.8% and a large reversible capacity of 359.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in the voltage range of 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V. Moreover, it showed a remarkable capacity of 242.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in low-voltage platform regions (≤0.15&nbsp;V) and maintained a high capacity retention of 91.4% after 100 cycles. Additionally, DFT models of various closed pore sizes ranging from 0.0 to 0.9&nbsp;nm have been rationally constructed and revealed that the HC material with a closed pore size of 0.45&nbsp;nm has the best Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption ability. Moreover, the as-assembled full cell of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//PCHC-10 delivered superior rate performance and a high energy density of 231.2&nbsp;Wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>. These exceptional electrochemical properties highlight the potential of PCHC-10 as a promising anode material for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403642-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403642-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Detailed decomposition temperature analyses for different raw materials, including pitch, phenolic resin, and pre-oxidized phenolic resin have been performed by using a thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) device to determine the optimal pre-oxidizing and carbonization temperatures. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), phenolic resin delivered a small weight loss within the range of 25–155&nbsp;°C, which corresponds to the water removal in phenolic resin. Subsequently, an obvious weight loss occurred within 155–380&nbsp;°C, which can be attributed to the fact that methylene and ether bonds connected to the phenolic resin skeleton began to break, resulting in small molecules of gas escaping from the carbon structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0017_R_d57147883e654" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Battery Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the weight of pre-oxidized phenolic resin remained almost unchanged before 250&nbsp;°C, except for water removal at 25–90&nbsp;°C, suggesting that pre-oxidized phenolic resin has more stable structure than that of phenolic resin. The TGA curve of pitch showed excellent thermal stability below 350&nbsp;°C, followed by a rapid weight loss until 560&nbsp;°C, which could be mainly ascribed to the release of numerous volatile compounds including CO and CH<sub>4</sub>. Moreover, the carbon yield of pitch following calcination at 900&nbsp;°C was 65.8%, higher than that of phenolic resins (49.9%) and pre-oxidized phenolic resins (55.6%). This result implies that adding pitch to pre-oxidized phenolic resins offers a cost-effective advantage. After that, a detailed schematic diagram was constructed to illustrate the synthesis process of PCHC-x materials with abundant and suitable-sized (0.45&nbsp;nm) closed pores and nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1a</a>). Initially, phenolic resin was pre-oxidation at 250&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h in a muffle furnace to fully achieve polymerization crosslinking reactions among phenolic resin oligomers, thereby effectively introducing abundant -COOH groups into the pre-oxidized phenolic resins. Besides, the pre-oxidation process is beneficial for relieving the rapid release of small molecule gases at high temperatures, thus stabilizing the graphite-like structure and reducing the specific surface area (SSA) of the final HC product to a comparable extent. Nevertheless, numerous open pore defects still persist on the HC surface during the high-temperature calcination process, which is harmful to the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability at low-voltage platform regions and the improvement of ICE. In order to address this issue, different amounts of petroleum pitch with abundant -OH groups were homogenously mixed with the pre-oxidized phenolic resin, and followed by a facile calcination process. During this process, a chemical crosslink reaction occurred between the -COOH groups in pre-oxidized phenolic resin and the -OH groups in pitch, resulting in the formation of abundant ester groups (-C(O)O-) that are tightly connected the nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer with the HC material. The chemical crosslink reaction increased the disorder degree and generated rich suitable-sized closed pore structures in the HC, thereby enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity at low-voltage plateau regions. Additionally, the nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer can effectively reduce open pore defects on the HC materials, thereby improving electrical conductivity and ICE. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) analyses were conducted to explore the intricate micromorphological features of both HC and PCHC-x materials. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a–h</a> (Supporting Information), all samples predominantly consisted of irregular bulk particles ranging from 1–25&nbsp;µm. With an increase in the pitch content from 5 to 20 wt%, the PCHC-x samples exhibited slightly smaller particle sizes and a rougher surface compared to HC. This observation suggests that the soft carbon precursor derived from pitch successfully coats the surface of pre-oxidized phenolic resin through a chemical crosslink reaction and can effectively alleviate the severe agglomeration of pre-oxidized phenolic resin. Moreover, the corresponding soft carbon coating layer thickness for PCHC-x samples is 1.4 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1c</a>), 2.9 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1d</a>), and 4.6&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1e</a>) when the pitch content is 5, 10 and 20 wt%, respectively. The internal part of HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1b</a>) exhibited obvious long-range ordered graphitic phase structures. In contrast, all PCHC-x samples predominantly contained abundant short-range ordered pseudo-graphite phase structures, indicating that the introduction of pitch effectively hinders the formation of ordered graphite phase structures during the carbonization process. Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) was employed to detect the existence forms of function groups on the surface of phenolic resin, pre-oxidized phenolic resin, and pitch. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), both the phenolic resin and pre-oxidized phenolic resin exhibited characteristic peaks corresponding to the stretching vibrations of hydroxyl groups at 1007 and 3461&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. The stretching vibration peaks representing the aromatic skeleton were found at 1600 and 1450&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, while the stretching vibrations of -C─O─C- bonds were detected at 1100 and 1236&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, out-of-plane peaks corresponding to Ar─H on the benzene ring appeared at 753 and 810&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>. After pre-oxidation, the intensities of the -C═C-, -C─O─C-, and Ar─H vibrational bonds in the phenolic resin decreased, and the vibrational peaks corresponding to -CH<sub>2</sub>- and R-CH<sub>2</sub>OH bonds at 2920, 2860, and 1007&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> disappeared. Furthermore, a -C═O vibrational peak at 1645&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> emerged in the pre-oxidized phenolic resin, indicating the successful oxidation of alcohol hydroxyl and phenol hydroxyl groups in the phenolic resin to carboxyl groups. It can be clearly seen a prominent vibrational peak of the hydroxyl group at 3461&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the FTIR spectrum of the pitch, indicating the presence of abundant aromatic phenolic structures in the pitch. This characteristic feature is highly conducive to promoting the chemical crosslinking reaction between the pitch and the pre-oxidized phenolic resin, which contains abundant -COOH groups, leading to the formation of -C(O)O- in the modified HC materials. Additionally, numerous closed pore structures existed in the inside region of PCHC-5, PCHC-10, and PCHC-20 samples, which can be observed from HRTEM images. According to the Nano measure software, the content and size of the closed pores in the fixed area of the HRTEM images were counted. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0001">1g</a>, the corresponding average closed pore sizes were 0.334, 0.446, and 0.376&nbsp;nm, respectively, which is significantly larger than that of HC (0.246&nbsp;nm). This phenomenon can primarily be attributed to the chemical crosslinking reaction between the -OH groups in pitch and the -COOH groups in pre-oxidized phenolic resin, leading to the formation of abundant ester groups and the subsequent generation of closed pore structures at high calcination temperatures. It is worth noting that PCHC-10 underwent the most extensive chemical crosslinking reaction due to an appropriate -OH content, thereby forming numerous closed pore structures with suitable pore sizes (0.446&nbsp;nm).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403642-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d1d60d4-7d92-41ba-a77e-ad5aa42cc7f7/adfm202403642-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d1d60d4-7d92-41ba-a77e-ad5aa42cc7f7/adfm202403642-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a571fd36-cb16-40a2-a7a0-5b64cd641a50/adfm202403642-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d1d60d4-7d92-41ba-a77e-ad5aa42cc7f7/adfm202403642-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403642-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The schematic diagram of the synthesis route of HC and PCHC-x. b–e) TEM images of HC, PCHC-5, PCHC-10, and PCHC-20. f) FTIR of pitch, phenolic resin, and pre-oxidized phenolic resin. g) The pore-size distribution curves for PCHC-x samples compared with HC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The detailed crystal structures of both HC and PCHC-x materials were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD). As depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2a</a>, all the samples exhibited characteristic broad peaks at 23° and 44°, which can be attributed to the 002 and 100 diffraction peaks of amorphous carbon. The HC material showed a relatively sharp peak shape, with an interlayer spacing calculated to be 0.349&nbsp;nm by Bragg's equation. This indicates a relatively ordered lattice structure in HC, which is consistent with TEM observations. With increasing pitch content, the 002 peak of PCHC-x samples showed a gradual broadening and shift to smaller angles compared to HC. This change arises from the abundant ester groups generated by the chemical cross-linking reaction, hindering the formation of ordered graphite microcrystalline structures. Consequently, this process results in an increase in the interlayer spacing of the PCHC-x materials during the carbonization process. The d<sub>002</sub> results calculated from Bragg's equation indicated an increase in the interlayer spacing of PCHC-x materials from 0.349 to 0.367&nbsp;nm with the increasing of pitch content (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). To further explore the microcrystalline structure of HC and PCHC-x materials, the 002 peak fitting was also performed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0018_R_d57147883e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The microcrystalline structure analysis of HC reveals a composition consisting of 56.45% graphite-like phases and 43.55% pseudo-graphitic phases, with respective interlayer spacings of 0.350 and 0.398&nbsp;nm. It is evident that all PCHC-x samples with varying soft carbon coating thickness exhibit a higher percentage of pseudo-graphitic phase compared to HC material. Apparently, all the PCHC-x samples with different soft carbon coating thickness have delivered more pseudo-graphitic phase percentage compared with HC material. This is attributed to the chemical cross-linking reaction between pitch and pre-oxidized phenolic resin, which introduces more closed pore structures into the carbon frameworks. Additionally, the pseudo-graphitic phase percentage in PCHC-10 is 60.43%, exceeding that in PCHC-20 (56.26%). This difference suggests that an excessive addition of pitch impedes the transformation from the graphite-like phase to the pseudo-graphitic phase, thereby diminishing the quantity of closed pores and hindering Na<sup>+</sup> storage in SIBs. The Raman patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2b</a> demonstrated that all the as-prepared materials exhibit characteristic broad D and G peaks at 1345 and 1600&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to the graphite structure and amorphous carbon structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0019_R_d57147883e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Raman spectra were further fitted to four subpeaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information) using the Lorentz function. The fitted peaks at 1200, 1340, 1500, and 1580&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to the D<sub>4</sub>, D<sub>1</sub>, D<sub>3</sub>, and G bands, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0020_R_d57147883e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The D<sub>3</sub> band represents the vibrational response of short-range sp<sup>3</sup>-C in the amorphous carbon material, while the D<sub>4</sub> band is associated with the stretching vibrations of heteroatoms in sp<sup>2</sup>-sp<sup>3</sup> hybridized structures or graphene terminal edges. In addition, the degree of disorder was assessed by determining the corresponding I<sub>D1</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio through the calculation of the area ratio of the D<sub>1</sub> and G bands. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the I<sub>D1</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of PCHC-5 (1.75) is slightly higher than that of HC (1.69) with a 5 wt% addition of pitch. This suggests that the PCHC-5 sample contains more pseudo-graphite phase structures, attributed to the abundant closed pores generated by the chemical crosslinking reaction. However, as the amount of pitch added was raised to 10 wt% and 20 wt%, the I<sub>D1</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratios correspondingly decreased to 1.57 and 1.21, respectively, compared to HC. This can be attributed to the increased thickness of the soft carbon coating derived from the pitch precursor, which enhances the ordered graphite layer structure on the surface of HC and reduces surface open pore defects. The values of I<sub>D1</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> initially rising and then decreasing demonstrate the successful introduction of a significant number of closed pore structures within the nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer through the chemical cross-linking reaction. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2c,d</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the HC material delivered a higher SSA and pore volume of 2.32 and 0.0237&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, compared with PCHC-x. This is primarily attributed to the decomposition of oxygen-containing functional groups in the pre-oxidized phenolic resin, which releases more small molecular gas during the carbonization process, leading to the formation of mesoporous structures. In stark contrast, the PCHC-x samples all exhibited lower SSA and pore volume compared to HC, demonstrating that the nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer can significantly reduce the open pore defective structures. Particularly, the corresponding SSA and pore volume of PCHC-10 were as low as 1.00&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 0.0129&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is advantageous in enhancing the ICE value during the first charging and discharging process. It is widely recognized that N<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements are limited to detecting open pore defect structures on the surface of materials. To better characterize the closed pore structures existing inside the HC materials, small angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) and true density tests were conducted. The SAXS analysis revealed a distinct hump structure at the scattering vector Q of 0.1–0.3&nbsp;Å in the PCHC samples (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a>, Supporting Information), which can be calculated by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0021_R_d57147883e859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nat. Commun.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
            
            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403642-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/22da2869-0523-4d84-95fe-4c86232d3fc7/adfm202403642-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0001.png">

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where λ = 1.541 Å is the X-ray wavelength, and θ is half the scattering angle. The humps are attributed to closed pores in the carbon matrix, including both micron-sized and nanosized pores located between the sp<sup>2</sup> graphite layers. Notably, PCHC-10 has a stronger peak intensity at 0.2&nbsp;Å, indicating a higher volume content of closed pores in PCHC-10 resulting from the deepest cross-linking reaction of -COOH groups on pre-oxidized phenolic resin with the -OH groups on pitch during the carbonation process. These findings align with the observed decrease in open pore volume in N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption measurements, which could be attributed to the fact that the pitch coating layer covers the open pore structures on the pre-oxidized phenolic resin surface to form more closed pore structures. The true density test is a common method for evaluating total closed pore volume and solid content, which can effectively exclude the open pore volume and interparticle spacing. The closed pore volume can be calculated using Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0022_R_d57147883e891" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv.          </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403642-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/07122734-f8d6-4477-b896-868eb57ec6a3/adfm202403642-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/07122734-f8d6-4477-b896-868eb57ec6a3/adfm202403642-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f2c484cb-f292-4335-8229-0022c68e466e/adfm202403642-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/07122734-f8d6-4477-b896-868eb57ec6a3/adfm202403642-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403642-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Raman spectra, and c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isothermal curves of HC and PCHC-x materials. d) the corresponding pore size distributions of HC and PCHC-x materials, inset: enlarged pore size distribution of PCHC-x. e) XPS survey spectra and the high-resolution O 1s spectra of f) HC, g) PCHC-5, h) PCHC-10 and i) PCHC-20.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In which, the value of 2.26&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> is derived from the true density of graphite, considered an ideal crystal with no closed pore structure. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the corresponding true densities of PCHC coated with increasing pitch addition (5-20 wt%) exhibit a change tendency of decrease initially and increase afterward, all being lower than that of pure HC (1.617&nbsp;g&nbsp;m<sup>−3</sup>). Conversely, the corresponding closed pore volumes display a change tendency of increase initially and decrease afterward, yet they remain significantly higher than that of pure HC (0.176&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Notably, the PCHC-10 sample displays the lowest true density of 1.505&nbsp;g&nbsp;m<sup>−3</sup> and the highest closed pore volume of 0.222&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the deepest degree of chemical cross-linking reaction when adding 10 wt% pitch. Therefore, the above results clearly illustrate that the chemical cross-linking reaction between the pre-oxidized phenolic resin and pitch transforms surface defective structures from open-pore to closed-pore, resulting in a higher closed-pore volume. To further investigate the variances in surface properties among the synthesized materials, X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) analysis was conducted to distinguish the configurations of C and O atoms. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2e</a>, all samples exhibited two elemental peaks corresponding to O1s (532.3&nbsp;eV) and C1s (284.7&nbsp;eV). The C and O content of PCHC-5 was determined to be 92.56% and 7.44%, respectively, corresponding to a C/O ratio of 12.44, slightly higher than that of the HC material (C/O = 11.56). This suggests that a successful chemical crosslinking reaction occurred between the pre-oxidized phenolic resin and pitch, leading to the consumption of O atoms as ester groups formed during the carbonization process. With the addition of pitch increased to 10 wt%, the C and O content of PCHC-10 was found to be 92.28% and 7.72%, respectively, resulting in a C/O ratio of 11.95. This ratio is slightly lower than that of PCHC-5 but significantly higher than that of PCHC-20 (C/O = 9.04), suggesting that the excessive addition of pitch did not affect the degree of the chemical crosslinking reaction. In other words, the addition of only 10 wt% pitch containing -OH groups are sufficient to entirely consume the -COOH groups in pre-oxidized phenolic resin for the formation of ester groups during the chemical crosslinking reaction. To better determine the existence type of oxygen-containing functional groups in HC and PCHC-x materials, the O 1s spectra can be fitted to O-I peak (533.7&nbsp;eV), O-II peak (532.2&nbsp;eV), and O-III peak (531.1&nbsp;eV). The O-I peak corresponds to carboxyl and ester groups (-COOH, -COOR), the O-II peak corresponds to phenol, hydroxyl and ether groups (C─OH, ─C─O─C-), and the O-III peak is related to carbonyl groups (C═O).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0018_R_d57147883e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The O 1s spectra of HC, PCHC-5, PCHC-10, and PCHC-20 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0002">2f–i</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) showed that PCHC-10 has the highest O-I peak percentage (47.4%) compared to HC (42.8%), PCHC-5 (44.5%) and PCHC-20 (41.3%), indicating a deeper chemical crosslinking reaction between pre-oxidized phenolic resin and pitch to form more ester groups when adding 10 wt% pitch. In addition, C1s spectra for HC and PCHC-x samples were fitted into four characteristic peaks, including C─C (284.6&nbsp;eV), C─OH (286.5&nbsp;eV), ─COOR (288.7&nbsp;eV) and C─OR (285.2&nbsp;eV) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, PCHC-10 exhibited the highest content of ester groups (16.3%) compared to HC (8.0%), PCHC-5 (12.5%) and PCHC-20 (16.1%), further demonstrating that incorporating just 10 wt% pitch with -OH groups is sufficient to completely utilize the -COOH groups present in pre-oxidized phenolic resin.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of the as-assembled PCHC-x and HC half cells were comprehensively evaluated. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3a</a> illustrates that all PCHC-x anodes exhibited higher average reversible capacity and superior rate performance compared to the HC anode. Especially, the PCHC-10 anode exhibited an impressive average specific capacity of 359.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly surpassing that of the HC (272.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), PCHC-5 (290.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and PCHC-20 (292.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) anode within the voltage range of 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V. Moreover, the PCHC-10 anode delivered a notable reversible capacity of 103.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at a higher current density of 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly superior to that of the HC anode (51.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), highlighting its excellent rate performance. In addition, the average specific capacity of PCHC-10 anode remained almost unchanged when the current density was recovered to 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating an outstanding reversibility in Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity. The galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves in the initial two cycles for the PCHC-10 anode at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) showed the typical high-voltage slope region (≥ 0.15&nbsp;V) and low-voltage plateau region (≤ 0.15&nbsp;V), corresponding to Na<sup>+</sup> adsorbed on the surfaces, edges and defective structures, as well as Na<sup>+</sup> filling in the interlayer and closed pore structures, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0023_R_d57147883e1029" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Energy Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial two cycles of GCD curves for the PCHC-10 anode almost overlapped, showing a higher degree of overlap compared to the HC anode, indicating superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage reversibility. Additionally, PCHC-10 exhibited an impressive initial discharge and charge-specific capacity of 481.1 and 359.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to a higher ICE value of 74.8% compared to the HC anode (59.3%, Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). This is mainly attributed to the fact that the nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer significantly reduces the open pore defect structures on the HC surface, thereby forming fewer and more stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films during the initial GCD process. Cycling stability tests of various anodes at a current rate of 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) revealed that all PCHC-x anodes exhibited higher specific capacity and greater capacity retention rates (&gt;90%) compared to the HC anode. Notably, the PCHC-10 anode demonstrated a final specific capacity as high as 355&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles, corresponding to a superior specific capacity retention of 91.4%, significantly exceeding that of the HC anode (85.4%), indicating exceptional cycling stability. The GCD curves of the 1st, 50th, and 100th cycles indicated that the specific capacity loss of the HC anode mainly occurred at the low-voltage platforms. In stark contrast, the specific capacity decline from 1st to 100th cycle for the PCHC-10 anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9b</a>, Supporting Information) at the low-voltage platforms region was significantly smaller than that of the HC anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a</a>, Supporting Information), indicating better Na<sup>+</sup> storage stability. This can be attributed to the ester-based bonds formed between pre-oxidized phenolic resin and pitch within the PCHC-10 sample through a chemical cross-linking reaction to form abundant and suitable-sized closed pore structures, providing more Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites at the low-voltage plateau regions. The GCD curves of different half-cells (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3c</a>) within 0.001–2.5&nbsp;V at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> indicate that the discharge capacity of the PCHC-10 anode at the low-voltage plateaus and sloping voltage regions is 248.2 and 117&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, significantly surpassing that of the HC (178.8 and 93.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), PCHC-5 (191.3 and 99.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), PCHC-20 anode (186.8 and 105.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and previously reported results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). At the current density of 60&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the charge-specific capacity of the PCHC-10 anode at the low-voltage plateaus and sloping regions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3d</a>) is 239.2 and 94.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, also significantly exceeding that of HC (145.0 and 76.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), PCHC-5 (206.9 and 83.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and PCHC-20 anode (212.8 and 91.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). Furthermore, at higher current densities (150 and 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), the charge capacity at the low-voltage plateau of the PCHC-10 anode outperformed that of the other anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3e,f</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Briefly, the corresponding charge-specific capacity ratios of PCHC-10 to HC anode at the low-voltage plateau region below 0.15&nbsp;V are significantly higher than those of PCHC-5 and PCHC-20 electrodes at the current densities of 30–300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3g</a>). This can mainly be ascribed to the fact that the PCHC-10 sample has a greater closed pore volume compared to other electrodes, thus providing a better Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability at the low plateau region. Even at 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the PCHC-10 anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0003">3h</a>) still delivers a higher reversible charge specific capacity of 103.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> than that of the HC (51.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) due to the suitable closed pore size and nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer existing in the PCHC-10 sample, which can effectively buffer the volume expansion and electrochemical polarization for the electrode at a high current density. Furthermore, the rate performance of PCHC-10 at a high mass loading of 4.5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> is also tested and illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a,b</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, PCHC-10 with 4.5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> retains a substantial reversible capacity of 323.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, with a high plateau capacity contribution of 220.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, delivering an excellent application potential for commercialization in SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403642-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ca4bac6f-b0c3-4ad2-bb6b-ec8db11df762/adfm202403642-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ca4bac6f-b0c3-4ad2-bb6b-ec8db11df762/adfm202403642-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3bf6adad-f153-4340-8049-50252fce56f0/adfm202403642-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ca4bac6f-b0c3-4ad2-bb6b-ec8db11df762/adfm202403642-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403642-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of HC and PCHC-x anodes. a) Rate performance at current densities ranging from 30 to 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Cycling performance at 30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. GCD curves at c) 30, d) 60, e) 150, and f) 300&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. g) Charge capacities ratio of PCHC-x to HC below 0.15&nbsp;V. h) GCD curves at 600&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of both HC and PCHC-10 anode at 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> in the initial two cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) exhibited an obvious reduction peak (B1) ≈ 1.15&nbsp;V, corresponding to the formation process of the SEI film. Interestingly, the bulge in the first CV curve of the PCHC-10 anode was smaller compared to that of the HC anode, indicating that fewer and more stable SEI films were generated. This can be attributed to the presence of a nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer on the surface of PCHC-10, which effectively reduces the open pore defective structures in the HC material, providing stable Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites and enhancing kinetic reversibility. These phenomena are consistent with the results obtained from the initial GCD curve. In addition, the larger CV curve area of the PCHC-10 anode compared to the HC anode indicates its superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability. CV measurements were conducted at various scan rates ranging from 0.1 to 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4a</a>) to further investigate the reaction kinetics of the PCHC-10 anode. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and capacitance-controlled behaviors for classical HC materials can be deduced from Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0024_R_d57147883e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. Sci.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403642-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8bd12b04-8342-4e4b-a00a-398b68297f3e/adfm202403642-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0003.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{{v}^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>i</i> and <i>v</i> represent the peak current and scan rate, respectively. The value of <i>b</i> can be calculated from the slopes of log (<i>i</i>) versus log (<i>v</i>), which are indicative of the contribution of Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms. A value of <i>b</i> close to 0.5 suggests that the electrochemical reaction is primarily diffusion-controlled, while a value of <i>b</i> close to 1.0 indicates that capacitance-controlled behavior dominates. The fitted values of b based on the oxidation peak (Peak 1, 0.25&nbsp;V) and the reduction peak (Peak 2, 0.003&nbsp;V) for PCHC-10 were 0.42 and 0.47, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4b</a>), suggesting that the electrochemical behavior at the low-voltage plateau is predominantly diffusion-controlled.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0025_R_d57147883e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relative contributions of capacitance-controlled and diffusion-controlled processes in CV curves at various scan rates can be further quantified by Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>).

            <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403642-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/89471b3e-0bac-483a-881b-a4c8828b54c2/adfm202403642-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0004.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>

                              </mrow>

                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i(V) = k1v + k2{{v}^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>where <i>ν</i> represents the scan rates, <i>k<sub>1</sub>ν</i> and <i>k<sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup></i> denote the contributions of capacitance-controlled and diffusion-controlled capacity, respectively. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4c</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), it is evident that the surface-controlled reaction tends to increase with rising scan rates. The capacitance contribution in the PCHC-10 anode rises from 38.3% at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to 68.7% at 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a shift in the main Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism within the 0.1–1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> range from intercalation and pore-filling to surface adsorption behavior. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed to analyze the impact of closed pore size and nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer on the electrochemical kinetics. As shown in the EIS Nyquist plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), the charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) at the interface between the anode materials and electrolyte of the PCHC-x anodes is observed to be lower than that of the HC anode (334.6&nbsp;Ω), suggesting that the nano-scale soft carbon coating layer significantly enhances the electrical conductivity of the HC material. Particularly, the PCHC-10 electrode exhibits the lowest R<sub>ct</sub> value (260.9&nbsp;Ω) compared to that of the PCHC-5 (302.1&nbsp;Ω) and PCHC-20 (299.4&nbsp;Ω) electrodes. This is attributed to the presence of a suitable nano-scaled soft coating layer and abundant closed pore size of 0.45&nbsp;nm in the PCHC-10 sample, which collectively enhances Na<sup>+</sup> electrochemical reaction kinetics during the charging and discharging processes. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) can be calculated by Equations&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>) and (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0026_R_d57147883e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J.          <div class="paragraph-element">In Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>), ω and σ denote the angular frequency in the low-frequency region and the Warburg impedance coefficient, respectively, and σ is associated with the slope of the fitting line of Z’ versus ω<sup>−0.5</sup>. In Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a>), R represents the gas constant, T signifies the absolute temperature, A stands for the electrode surface area, n denotes the number of electrons per mole of active material involved in the electrochemical reaction process, F indicates the Faraday's constant, and C<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> represents the Na<sup>+</sup> molar concentration. As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the calculated <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6906fb5f-21f4-46ea-a4c8-af9911d84d18/adfm202403642-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0007.png">

In addition, the apparent <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b93662f5-98d4-40ab-95dc-e0448b09c838/adfm202403642-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0009.png">

As exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4f</a>, the pristine recorded GITT curves of PCHC-10 and HC anodes are highly in accord with their GCD curves. Additionally, the corresponding <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8a1c35c9-a404-4d75-b69e-c72b1d46ced4/adfm202403642-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0010.png">

Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403642-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>In the equations, τ represents the pulse duration, m<sub>B</sub> and M<sub>B</sub> are the active mass and molar mass of carbon, V<sub>m</sub> refers the molar volume, and S is the electrode-electrolyte contact area. Additionally, the schematic of ΔE<sub>s</sub> corresponding to the potential change caused by pulse and the ΔE<sub>t</sub> corresponding to the potential change of constant current charging (discharging) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). During the sodiation and desodiation processes of the PCHC-10 and HC anodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4g</a>), their <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/1a88fa96-89ec-438d-a42b-8b6cd2e7e6bd/adfm202403642-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0012.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> values exhibited a similar trend with a significant change within the range of 0–0.1&nbsp;V during the Na<sup>+</sup> charge and discharge process, indicating the occurrence of phase transformation at low-voltage platforms. Moreover, the slight change observed around 0.65&nbsp;V may be attributed to the formation of SEI films and the shift in Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism from surface or defect adsorption to interlayer adsorption and insertion. The PCHC-10 anode exhibited a higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c6ad70ba-2193-42f4-955e-eebdc4e8bde3/adfm202403642-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0013.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{N{{a}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> value than the HC anode throughout the entire charge and discharge process, indicating that the presence of nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer and an appropriately sized closed pore of 0.45&nbsp;nm formed by chemical cross-linking reaction between the pre-oxidized phenolic resin and pitch significantly enhanced the diffusion kinetics, which is highly consistent with the EIS results. Based on the above results, the detailed Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism for PCHC-10 can be summarized by highlighting the correlation between its microstructures and electrochemical performance. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0004">4h</a>, numerous Na<sup>+</sup> were first adsorbed on the surface or defect structures of PCHC-10 at potentials ranging from 0.65 to 2.5&nbsp;V. Subsequently, the Na<sup>+</sup> were adsorbed on the edge of graphite interlayer around 0.1–0.65&nbsp;V, and then Na<sup>+</sup> were inserted into the graphite interlayer and gradually filled the inner surfaces below 0.1&nbsp;V. As the discharge depth increased, sodium metal clusters were formed via sodium-sodium interactions, aggregating to nucleate and store in size-suitable closed pore structures of 0.45&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0023_R_d57147883e1689" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Energy Chem.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon is well consistent with the observation that the lowest Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients were generated in the low plateau regions. Therefore, the superior electrochemical performance of the PCHC-10 anode can be primarily attributed to the following points: 1) The ester functional groups formed by chemical cross-linking reaction of -COOH groups in pre-oxidized phenolic resin with -OH groups in pitch produced abundant closed pore structures during carbonization process, which provides more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage under low-voltage platform region. 2) The optimized PCHC-10 sample has an optimal closed pore size of 0.45&nbsp;nm, which can significantly enhance the transport rate of Na<sup>+</sup> under a low-voltage platform region. 3) The nano-scaled soft carbon coating layer on PCHC-10 generated more closed pore structures and reduced the surface defect structures, thereby improving the ICE and electrical conductivity. 4) The PCHC-10 sample has a larger interlayer spacing than that of pure HC, which can effectively offer abundant Na<sup>+</sup> storage room and reduce the diffusion energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation, thereby improving the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity and cycling performance in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the closed relationship between the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism and the closed pore sizes in the PCHC-10 sample. Density functional theoretical (DFT) calculations were carried out to investigate the ability of carbon interlayer with various pore sizes to capture Na<sup>+</sup>. After that, seven bilayer-carbon structure models with pore sizes ranging from 0.00 to ≈0.90&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) were rationally constructed using first-principles calculation. The Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption sites were determined by the defect vacancies on the carbon interlayer structures, and the adsorption energy (<i>E<sub>ads</sub></i>) was calculated using the equation of <i>Eads</i> = <i>ECNa</i> − <i>E</i>C − <i>ENa</i>. Where <i>E<sub>CNa</sub></i> and <i>E<sub>C</sub></i> represent the total energy of the system after and before the introduction of Na<sup>+</sup>, respectively, and <i>E<sub>Na</sub></i> is the energy per atom for the bulk metal. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0005">5a</a><b>–g</b> depicts the carbon interlayer configurations for capturing Na<sup>+</sup>, while Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information) presents the corresponding <i>E<sub>ads</sub></i> and interlayer spacing. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0005">5h</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy showed an initial increase followed by a decrease as the pore size of the carbon interlayer structures varied from 0.00 to ≈0.90&nbsp;nm. Among the seven investigated models, the C4 configuration featuring a pore size of ≈0.45&nbsp;nm displayed the most excellent <i>E<sub>ads</sub></i> value (−4.71&nbsp;eV), indicating a superior potential for single Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. Furthermore, in comparison to other defect size models, Na<sup>+</sup> accommodated in the defect size model of ≈0.45&nbsp;nm appears a more stable structure, presumably attributable to the presence of six edge carbons to Na<sup>+</sup> on the defect edge. Specifically, when the pore size was below 0.45&nbsp;nm (C1–C3), the adsorption ability of the carbon interlayer structure for Na<sup>+</sup> was weakened compared with C4 structure. However, it still managed to form a relatively stable chemical adsorption configuration based on the adsorption energy perspective. It could be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0005">5i</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the layer spacings of the carbon interlayer structure for C1–C6 were ≈ 0.367&nbsp;nm, exceeding that of the original graphite (≈0.34&nbsp;nm). The larger layer spacing could reduce the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation, thereby improving the Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0028_R_d57147883e1793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, with the gradual increase in pore size to 0.9&nbsp;nm, the adsorption capacity of the carbon interlayer structure decreased significantly, as evidenced by the <i>E<sub>ads</sub></i> of C7 rapidly dropping to −0.13&nbsp;eV, nearly losing the ability to capture Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition, in the C7 structure, the layer spacing of the carbon interlayer decreased to 0.34&nbsp;nm, approaching the original graphite interlayer spacing, thereby impeding the movement speed of Na<sup>+</sup> within the carbon interlayer.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403642-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b180f289-a25e-4e2d-aa89-9513870f90df/adfm202403642-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b180f289-a25e-4e2d-aa89-9513870f90df/adfm202403642-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7a9b9b35-7ea7-4e18-ba1c-dbc56194ad94/adfm202403642-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b180f289-a25e-4e2d-aa89-9513870f90df/adfm202403642-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403642-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403642" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The adsorption models of Na<sup>+</sup> on carbon interlayer structures with different pore sizes. The top view and front view of a) 0, b) 0.15, c) 0.35, d) 0.45, e) 0.55, f) 0.72, and g) 0.90&nbsp;nm. The corresponding h) adsorption energy and i) interlayer spacing of different adsorption models.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Encouraged by the outstanding Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance of the PCHC-10 half cell, sodium-ion full cells (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0006">6a</a>) were fabricated using a PCHC-10 anode and commercial Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode, denoted as NVP//PCHC-10. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0006">6b</a>, the specific average capacity of NVP//PCHC-10 are 109.1, 104.1, 95.5, 86.1, 75.7, and 67.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 3&nbsp;C within the voltage range of 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V, respectively. The corresponding specific capacity maintenance rate reaches 61.9% at 3&nbsp;C compared to 0.1&nbsp;C as a standard, demonstrating outstanding rate performance across the range from 0.1 to 3&nbsp;C. The average specific capacity still can remained at 103&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density was recovered to 0.1&nbsp;C, indicating excellent capacity reversibility. The initial GCD curves of the full cell at 0.1&nbsp;C delivered a high initial ICE of 95.4% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18b</a>, Supporting Information), slightly higher than that of NVP//HC (92.4%) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a</a>, Supporting Information), showing a good capacity reversibility and excellent commercial value. Additionally, all the GCD curves of NVP//PCHC-10 at various current densities ranging from 0.1 to 3&nbsp;C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0006">6c</a>) clearly showed extremely stable charge/discharge voltage plateaus within the range of 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V, indicating excellent electrochemical stability. Besides, the cycling performance of NVP//PCHC-10 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0006">6d</a>) delivered a high initial specific capacity of 103&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, ultimately stabilizing at 82.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;C after 100 cycles, and corresponding to 80% capacity retention rate, delivering a good cycling stability. Notably, the cycled full cell can successfully illuminated the CUP logo comprising 51 LEDs, further demonstrating superior Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability and robust cycling stability, suggesting promising practical application potential. Both the GCD curves of NVP//PCHC-10 at 50th and 100th still exhibit stable working voltage within 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403642-fig-0006">6e</a>), with shapes similar to that of 1st, further demonstrating the excellent cycling performance of the full cell. Furthermore, the corresponding energy density (E, W&nbsp;h&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) and power density (P, W&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup>) for the as-assembled full cell can be calculated by Equations (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403642-disp-0008" title="Link to equation">8-10</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403642-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403642-bib-0029_R_d57147883e1886" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403642-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0197ff26-cb05-45d9-9ff4-9774d25139c0/adfm202403642-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403642:adfm202403642-math-0014" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403642-math-0014.png">
